					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Yellow citrus fruit</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the fruit. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Lemon_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Lemon (disambiguation)">Lemon (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Lemons" redirects here. For the surname, see <a href="/wiki/Lemons_(surname)" title="Lemons (surname)">Lemons (surname)</a>.</div>

</td></tr>

</tbody></table><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1220493105">html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:white}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:white}}</style>
<p>The <b>lemon</b> (<b><i>Citrus</i> × <i>limon</i></b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> of small <a href="/wiki/Evergreen" title="Evergreen">evergreen</a> tree in the <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plant</a> family <a href="/wiki/Rutaceae" title="Rutaceae">Rutaceae</a>, native to <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, primarily <a href="/wiki/Northeast_India" title="Northeast India">Northeast India</a> (<a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a>), Northern Myanmar, and China.<sup id="cite_ref-morton_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morton-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The tree's <a href="/wiki/Ellipsoid" title="Ellipsoid">ellipsoidal</a> yellow <a href="/wiki/Hesperidium" title="Hesperidium">fruit</a> is used for culinary and non-culinary purposes throughout the world, primarily for its <a href="/wiki/Juice" title="Juice">juice</a>, which has both culinary and cleaning uses.<sup id="cite_ref-morton_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morton-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The pulp and <a href="/wiki/Peel_(fruit)" title="Peel (fruit)">rind</a> are also used in cooking and baking. The juice of the lemon is about 5–6% <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid" title="Citric acid">citric acid</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> with a <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> of around 2.2,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> giving it a sour taste. The distinctive sour taste of lemon juice, derived from the citric acid, makes it a key ingredient in <a href="/wiki/Drink" title="Drink">drinks</a> and foods<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Lemonade" title="Lemonade">lemonade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lemon_meringue_pie" title="Lemon meringue pie">lemon meringue pie</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h2>
<p>The lemon tree produces an <a href="/wiki/Ellipsoid" title="Ellipsoid">ellipsoidal</a> yellow <a href="/wiki/Hesperidium" title="Hesperidium">fruit</a>.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1021810730">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery{width:100%!important}}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery{display:table}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-default{background:transparent;margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-center{margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-left{float:left}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-right{float:right}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-none{float:none}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-collapsible{width:100%}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .title,.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .main,.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .footer{display:table-row}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .title>div{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0 0.6em 1.6em;text-align:center;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .main>div{display:table-cell}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .gallery{line-height:1.35em}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .footer>div{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0 0.6em 1.6em;text-align:right;font-size:80%;line-height:1em}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .title>div *,.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .footer>div *{overflow:visible}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .gallerybox img{background:none!important}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .bordered-images .thumb img{border:solid #eaecf0 1px}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .whitebg .thumb{background:#fff!important}</style><div class="mod-gallery mod-gallery-default mod-gallery-center"><div class="main"><div><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed nochecker bordered-images whitebg">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span></h3>
<p>Lemons contain numerous <a href="/wiki/Phytochemical" title="Phytochemical">phytochemicals</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Polyphenol" title="Polyphenol">polyphenols</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terpene" title="Terpene">terpenes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tannin" title="Tannin">tannins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Lemon juice contains slightly more <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid" title="Citric acid">citric acid</a> than <a href="/wiki/Lime_(fruit)" title="Lime (fruit)">lime</a> juice (about 47&#160;g/L), nearly twice the citric acid of <a href="/wiki/Grapefruit" title="Grapefruit">grapefruit</a> juice, and about five times the amount of citric acid found in <a href="/wiki/Orange_(fruit)" title="Orange (fruit)">orange</a> juice.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Citron#Origin_and_distribution" title="Citron">Citron §&#160;Origin and distribution</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Citrus_x_limon_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-041.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Citrus_x_limon_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-041.jpg/220px-Citrus_x_limon_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-041.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="273" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Citrus_x_limon_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-041.jpg/330px-Citrus_x_limon_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-041.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Citrus_x_limon_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-041.jpg/440px-Citrus_x_limon_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-041.jpg 2x" data-file-width="459" data-file-height="570" /></a><figcaption>Taxonomic illustration by <a href="/wiki/Franz_Eugen_K%C3%B6hler" class="mw-redirect" title="Franz Eugen Köhler">Franz Eugen Köhler</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>The origin of the lemon is unknown, though lemons are thought to have first grown in <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a> (a region in <a href="/wiki/Northeast_India" title="Northeast India">northeast India</a>), northern <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>, or <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-morton_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morton-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The source only states &quot;The true home of the lemon is unknown, though some have linked it to northwestern India.&quot; (May 2021)">failed verification</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> A genomic study of the lemon indicated it was a hybrid between <a href="/wiki/Bitter_orange" title="Bitter orange">bitter orange</a> (sour orange) and <a href="/wiki/Citron" title="Citron">citron</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The origin of the word <i>lemon</i> may be Middle Eastern.<sup id="cite_ref-morton_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morton-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The word draws from the Old French <i>limon</i>, then Italian <i>limone</i>, from the Arabic <i>laymūn</i> or <i>līmūn</i>, and from the <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> <i>līmūn</i>, a generic term for <a href="/wiki/Citrus" title="Citrus">citrus</a> fruit, which is a cognate of Sanskrit (<i>nimbū</i>, '<a href="/wiki/Lime_(fruit)" title="Lime (fruit)">lime</a>').<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Varieties">Varieties</span></h3>
<p>The 'Bonnie Brae' is oblong, smooth, thin-skinned, and seedless.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> These are mostly grown in <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_County" class="mw-redirect" title="San Diego County">San Diego County</a>, US.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 'Eureka' grows year-round and abundantly. This is the common supermarket lemon, also known as "Four Seasons" (<i>Quatre Saisons</i>) because of its ability to produce fruit and flowers together throughout the year. This variety is also available as a plant for domestic customers.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> There is also a <a href="/wiki/Pink-fleshed_Eureka_lemon" class="mw-redirect" title="Pink-fleshed Eureka lemon">pink-fleshed Eureka lemon</a> with a green and yellow <a href="/wiki/Variegated" class="mw-redirect" title="Variegated">variegated</a> outer skin.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Lisbon lemon is very similar to the Eureka and is the other common supermarket lemon. It is smoother than the Eureka, has thinner skin, and has fewer or no seeds. It generally produces more juice than the Eureka.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 'Femminello St. Teresa', or 'Sorrento'<sup id="cite_ref-latimes_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-latimes-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> originates in Italy. This fruit's <a href="/wiki/Zest_(ingredient)" title="Zest (ingredient)">zest</a> is high in lemon oils. It is the variety traditionally used in the making of <i><a href="/wiki/Limoncello" title="Limoncello">limoncello</a></i>.
</p><p>The 'Yen Ben' is an Australasian cultivar.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultivation">Cultivation</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h3>
<p>Lemons are supposed to have entered <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> near southern <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> no later than the second century AD, during the time of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Ancient Rome</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-morton_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morton-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> They were later introduced to <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persia</a> and then to <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> around 700 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-morton_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morton-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The lemon was first recorded in literature in a 10th-century <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> treatise on farming and was also used as an ornamental plant in early <a href="/wiki/Islamic_garden" title="Islamic garden">Islamic gardens</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-morton_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morton-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> It was distributed widely throughout the Arab world and the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_region" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean region">Mediterranean region</a> between 1000 and 1150.<sup id="cite_ref-morton_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morton-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> An article on Lemon and lime tree cultivation in <a href="/wiki/Andalusia" title="Andalusia">Andalusia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, is brought down in <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-%27Awwam" title="Ibn al-&#39;Awwam">Ibn al-'Awwam</a>'s 12th-century agricultural work, <i>Book on Agriculture</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first substantial cultivation of lemons in Europe began in <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a> in the middle of the 15th century. The lemon was later introduced to the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> in 1493, when <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> brought lemon seeds to <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a> on his voyages. Spanish conquest throughout the New World helped spread lemon seeds. It was mainly used as an ornamental plant and for medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-morton_2-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morton-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In the 19th century, lemons were increasingly planted in <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> and <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-morton_2-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morton-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1747, <a href="/wiki/James_Lind_(physician)" class="mw-redirect" title="James Lind (physician)">James Lind</a>'s experiments on seamen suffering from <a href="/wiki/Scurvy" title="Scurvy">scurvy</a> involved adding lemon juice to their diets, though <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a> was not yet known as an important dietary ingredient.<sup id="cite_ref-morton_2-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morton-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Propagation">Propagation</span></h3>
<p>Lemons need a minimum temperature of around 7&#160;°C (45&#160;°F), so they are not hardy year-round in temperate climates, but become hardier as they mature.<sup id="cite_ref-rhs_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rhs-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Citrus require minimal pruning by trimming overcrowded branches, with the tallest branch cut back to encourage bushy growth.<sup id="cite_ref-rhs_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rhs-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Throughout summer, pinching back tips of the most vigorous growth assures more abundant canopy development. As mature plants may produce unwanted, fast-growing shoots (called "water shoots"), these are removed from the main branches at the bottom or middle of the plant.<sup id="cite_ref-rhs_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rhs-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The tradition of <a href="/wiki/Urination" title="Urination">urinating</a> near a lemon tree<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
may be beneficial as urine is a fertilizer.
</p><p>In cultivation in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">UK</a>, the cultivars "Meyer"<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
and "Variegata"<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
have gained the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Horticultural_Society" title="Royal Horticultural Society">Royal Horticultural Society</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Award_of_Garden_Merit" title="Award of Garden Merit">Award of Garden Merit</a> (confirmed 2017).<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<p>In 2022, world production of lemons (combined with <a href="/wiki/Lime_(fruit)" title="Lime (fruit)">limes</a> for reporting) was 22 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> led by India with 18% of the total. Mexico and China were major secondary producers (table).

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition">Nutrition</span></h3>
<p>Lemon is a rich source of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a>, providing 64% of the <a href="/wiki/Daily_Value" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily Value">Daily Value</a> in a 100 g reference amount. Other <a href="/wiki/Essential_nutrient" class="mw-redirect" title="Essential nutrient">essential nutrients</a> are low in content.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culinary">Culinary</span></h3>
<p>Lemon juice, rind, and peel are used in a wide variety of foods and drinks. The whole lemon is used to make <a href="/wiki/Marmalade" title="Marmalade">marmalade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lemon_curd" class="mw-redirect" title="Lemon curd">lemon curd</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lemon_liqueur" title="Lemon liqueur">lemon liqueur</a>. Lemon slices and lemon rind are used as a <a href="/wiki/Garnish_(food)" title="Garnish (food)">garnish</a> for food and drinks. Lemon <a href="/wiki/Zest_(ingredient)" title="Zest (ingredient)">zest</a>, the grated outer rind of the fruit, is used to add flavor to baked goods, puddings, rice, and other dishes.
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1021810730"><div class="mod-gallery mod-gallery-default mod-gallery-center"><div class="main"><div><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed nochecker bordered-images whitebg">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 272px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 270px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Lemon_-_whole_and_split.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Lemon external surface and cross-section"><img alt="Lemon external surface and cross-section" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Lemon_-_whole_and_split.jpg/405px-Lemon_-_whole_and_split.jpg" decoding="async" width="270" height="180" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Lemon_-_whole_and_split.jpg/608px-Lemon_-_whole_and_split.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Lemon_-_whole_and_split.jpg/810px-Lemon_-_whole_and_split.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="4000" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Lemon external surface and cross-section</div>
		</li>
</ul></div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Juice">Juice</span></h4>
<p>Lemon juice is used to make <a href="/wiki/Lemonade" title="Lemonade">lemonade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soft_drink" title="Soft drink">soft drinks</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cocktail" title="Cocktail">cocktails</a>. It is used in <a href="/wiki/Marinade" class="mw-redirect" title="Marinade">marinades</a> for fish, where its acid neutralizes <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amines</a> in fish by converting them into nonvolatile <a href="/wiki/Ammonium" title="Ammonium">ammonium</a> salts. In meat, the acid partially <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolyzes</a> tough collagen fibers, <a href="/wiki/Tenderizing" class="mw-redirect" title="Tenderizing">tenderizing</a> it.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, lemon juice is frequently added to <a href="/wiki/Pancakes" class="mw-redirect" title="Pancakes">pancakes</a>, especially on <a href="/wiki/Shrove_Tuesday" title="Shrove Tuesday">Shrove Tuesday</a>.
</p><p>Lemon juice is also used as a short-term <a href="/wiki/Preservative" title="Preservative">preservative</a> on certain foods that tend to oxidize and turn brown after being sliced (<a href="/wiki/Enzymatic_browning" class="mw-redirect" title="Enzymatic browning">enzymatic browning</a>), such as <a href="/wiki/Apple" title="Apple">apples</a>, <a href="/wiki/Banana" title="Banana">bananas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Avocado" title="Avocado">avocados</a>, where its acid denatures the enzymes.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Peel">Peel</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, lemons are preserved in jars or barrels of salt. The salt penetrates the peel and rind, softening them, and curing them so that they last almost indefinitely.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The preserved lemon is used in a wide variety of dishes. Preserved lemons can also be found in Sicilian, Italian, Greek, and French dishes.
</p><p>The peel can be used in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Pectin" title="Pectin">pectin</a>, a polysaccharide used as a gelling agent and stabilizer in food and other products.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil">Oil</span></h4>
<p>Lemon oil is extracted from oil-containing cells in the skin. A machine breaks up the cells and uses a water spray to flush off the oil. The oil–water mixture is then filtered and separated by <a href="/wiki/Centrifugation" title="Centrifugation">centrifugation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Leaves">Leaves</span></h4>
<p>The leaves of the lemon tree are used to make a tea and for preparing cooked meats and seafoods.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses">Other uses</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial">Industrial</span></h4>
<p>Lemons were the primary commercial source of <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid" title="Citric acid">citric acid</a> before the development of <a href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a>-based processes.<sup id="cite_ref-Hofrichter2010_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hofrichter2010-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aroma">Aroma</span></h4>
<p>Lemon oil may be used in <a href="/wiki/Aromatherapy" title="Aromatherapy">aromatherapy</a>. Lemon oil aroma does not influence the human <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> but may contribute to relaxation.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h4>
<p>One educational science experiment involves attaching <a href="/wiki/Electrode" title="Electrode">electrodes</a> to a lemon and using it as a <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battery (electricity)">battery</a> to produce electricity. Although very low power, several <a href="/wiki/Lemon_battery" title="Lemon battery">lemon batteries</a> can power a small digital watch.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> These experiments also work with other fruits and vegetables.
</p><p>Lemon juice may be used as a simple <a href="/wiki/Invisible_ink" title="Invisible ink">invisible ink</a>, developed by heat.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lemon juice can be used to increase the <a href="/wiki/Blonde" class="mw-redirect" title="Blonde">blonde</a> colour of hair, acting as a natural highlight after the moistened hair is exposed to sunlight. This is due to the citric acid that acts as bleach.<sup id="cite_ref-GLAMOUR_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GLAMOUR-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Klorane_Labratories_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klorane_Labratories-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Other_citrus_called_.22lemons.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_citrus_called_&quot;lemons&quot;">Other citrus called "lemons"</span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Citrus_depressa" title="Citrus depressa">Flat lemon</a>, a mandarin hybrid.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Meyer_lemon" title="Meyer lemon">Meyer lemon</a>, a cross between a citron and a mandarin/pomelo hybrid distinct from sour or sweet orange,<sup id="cite_ref-limes_and_lemons_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-limes_and_lemons-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> named after Frank N. Meyer, who first introduced it to the United States in 1908. Thin-skinned and slightly less acidic than the Lisbon and Eureka lemons, Meyer lemons require more care when shipping and are not widely grown on a commercial basis. Meyer lemons often mature to a yellow-orange color. They are slightly more frost tolerant.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ponderosa_lemon" title="Ponderosa lemon">Ponderosa lemon</a>, more cold-sensitive than true lemons, the fruit are thick-skinned and very large. Genetic analysis showed it to be a complex hybrid of citron and pomelo.<sup id="cite_ref-limes_and_lemons_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-limes_and_lemons-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rough_lemon" title="Rough lemon">Rough lemon</a>, a citron-mandarin cross, cold-hardy and often used as a <a href="/wiki/Citrus_rootstock" title="Citrus rootstock">citrus rootstock</a><sup id="cite_ref-limes_and_lemons_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-limes_and_lemons-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sweet_lemon" title="Sweet lemon">Sweet lemons</a> or sweet limes, a mixed group including the <a href="/wiki/Lumia_(citrus)" title="Lumia (citrus)">lumia</a> (pear lemon), <a href="/wiki/Citrus_limetta" title="Citrus limetta">limetta</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_sweet_lime" title="Palestinian sweet lime">Palestinian sweet lime</a>. Among them is the Jaffa lemon, a pomelo-citron hybrid.<sup id="cite_ref-limes_and_lemons_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-limes_and_lemons-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Volkamer_lemon" title="Volkamer lemon">Volkamer lemon</a>, like the rough lemon, a citron-mandarin cross<sup id="cite_ref-limes_and_lemons_41-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-limes_and_lemons-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Jacob_Foppen_van_Es_-_Still_life_with_pitcher.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Jacob_Foppen_van_Es_-_Still_life_with_pitcher.jpg/170px-Jacob_Foppen_van_Es_-_Still_life_with_pitcher.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="119" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Jacob_Foppen_van_Es_-_Still_life_with_pitcher.jpg/255px-Jacob_Foppen_van_Es_-_Still_life_with_pitcher.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Jacob_Foppen_van_Es_-_Still_life_with_pitcher.jpg/340px-Jacob_Foppen_van_Es_-_Still_life_with_pitcher.jpg 2x" data-file-width="744" data-file-height="522" /></a><figcaption>Painting by <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Foppens_van_Es" title="Jacob Foppens van Es">Jacob Foppens van Es</a></figcaption></figure>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="14" class="mw-file-element" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></span></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Lemon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2023</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In India, a lemon may be ritually encircled around a person due to the belief that it would repel negative energies or ward off the <a href="/wiki/Evil_eye" title="Evil eye">evil eye</a>, called <i>nazar</i> or <i>drishti</i>. It is also a common practice for owners of a new car to drive over four lemons, one under each wheel, crushing them during their first drive. This is believed to protect the driver from accidents.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hindu_deities" title="Hindu deities">Hindu deities</a> are sometimes depicted with lemons in their <a href="/wiki/Hindu_iconography" title="Hindu iconography">iconography</a>, representing the attribute of wealth or abundance.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
